Everything Occurs by God's Will
Explains that all things occur by God's will, and human will is subordinate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's eternal will encompasses all things, including human actions, and that human will is subordinate yet real.

Core Idea
Nothing happens without God's will, yet humans still choose.

This passage from the Fiqh al-Akbar commentary explains the all-encompassing nature of divine will. It lists opposites to show that every category of existence is under God's decree. At the same time, it clarifies that human will is not negated, using Quranic evidence. Prepare to see how the author balances divine sovereignty and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Understand the meaning of God's willing another's action
The passage begins by defining divine will for another's act as commanding it. Recognize that God's will is eternal and applies to all things.
2
Observe the comprehensive list of opposites
The author lists pairs like good/evil, faith/disbelief, obedience/disobedience to show nothing is outside God's will.
Notice the poetic rhythm of the list.
3
Note the key phrase: 'Whatever God wills is'
This is a foundational statement of Sunni belief in qadar. It affirms God's absolute sovereignty.
4
See the argument from impossibility
All creation cannot move an atom without God's will. This emphasizes absolute divine power.
This illustrates the principle with an extreme example.
5
Recognize the compatibility with human will
The author cites two verses: one showing human will depends on God, another showing humans have will ('Do whatever you will'). Conclude that divine will does not negate human choice.
This resolves a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومعنى إرادته فعل غيره أنه أمر به فإنه تعالى مريد بإرادته القديمة ما كان وما يكون فلا يكون في الدنيا ولا في الأخرى صغير أو كبير، قليل أو كثير، خير أو شر، نفع أو ضر، حلو أو مُرٌّ، إيمان أو كفر، عرفان أو نكر، فوز أو خسران زيادة أو نقصان طاعة أو عصيان، إلا بإرادته ووفق حكمته وطبق تقديره وقضائه في خليقته. فما شاء الله كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن، فهو الفعّال لما يريد كما يريد، لا راد لما أراد ولا معقب لما حكم في العباد ولا مهرب عن معصيته إلا بإرادته ومعونته ولا مكسب لعبد في طاعته إلا بتوفيقه ومشيئته فلا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله ولا منجا ولا ملجأ منه إلا إليه، ولو اجتمع الخلق على أن يحركوا في العالم ذرّة أو يسكنوها مرة بدون إرادته لما قدروا على ذلك، بل ولا أرادوا خلاف ما هنالك، كما قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَمَاتَشَاءُونَ إِلَّا أَنْ تَشَاء اللَّهُ ﴾ [الإنسان: ٣٠]، فهو سبحانه لم يزل موصوفا بإرادته ومريدا في الأزل وجودَ الأشياء في أوقاتها التي قدرها فوجدت فيها كما علمها وأرادها وقدرها من غير تقدّم ولا تأخر وتبدل وتغيّر، وهذا لا ينافي أن يكون للعبد مشيئة لقوله: اعْمَلُوا مَا شِئْتُمْ ﴾ [فصلت: ٤٠] .
And the meaning of His willing the act of another is that He commands it. For He, exalted, is willing with His eternal will what was and what will be. So there is nothing in the world or the hereafter, small or large, little or much, good or evil, benefit or harm, sweet or bitter, faith or disbelief, knowledge or denial, success or loss, increase or decrease, obedience or disobedience, except by His will, in accordance with His wisdom, according to His decree and determination in His creation. So whatever God wills is, and whatever He does not will is not. He is the Doer of what He wills as He wills, no one can repel what He wills, nor object to what He decrees for His servants, nor escape from disobedience to Him except by His will and assistance, nor can any servant acquire anything in his obedience except by His granting success and His decree. So there is no strength or power except with God, and no refuge or shelter from Him except to Him. Even if all creation gathered to move an atom in the world or to make it still once without His will, they would not be able to do that, nor would they even will contrary to what is there, as God Almighty said: 'And you do not will except that Allah wills' [Al-Insan: 30]. So He, glorified, has never ceased to be described by His will, and He is willing in eternity the existence of things at their times which He determined, so they came into existence at those times as He knew them, willed them, and determined them, without advancement or delay, change or alteration. And this does not contradict that the servant has a will, because of His saying: 'Do whatever you will' [Fussilat: 40].
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the comprehensive scope of divine will. The author begins by explaining that God's will for another's action means He commands it. Then he affirms that everything—every size, quality, outcome, and action—occurs by God's will, wisdom, and decree. He lists opposites to show God's will encompasses all. The famous phrase 'Whatever God wills is, and whatever He does not will is not' is quoted. God is absolutely powerful and no one can oppose His will. Even if all creation tried to move an atom without His will, they would fail. Two Quranic verses are cited: one affirming that human will is dependent on God's will, and another showing that humans have a will ('Do whatever you will'). The author concludes that human will does not contradict divine will.

مشيئة
قضاء

توفيق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine will negates human free will entirely.
Correction: The author shows that human will is affirmed by the verse 'Do whatever you will,' and divine will does not contradict it.

Key Takeaway
God's eternal will governs all things, but human will is real and not negated.

Quranic Proof for Divine Will: Irada and Mashi'a Are One in God
Cites Quranic verses and states divine unity of irada and mashi'a.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Quran provides evidence for the attributes of irada and mashi'a, and that these are considered one and the same in relation to God Almighty.

Core Idea
For God, will and decree are identical; for humans, they differ.

In this passage, the author cites two Quranic verses to establish the divine attributes of will and decree. He then clarifies that while these terms are used interchangeably for God, they have distinct meanings when applied to human actions. This lesson focuses on the Quranic proof and the divine unity of the two terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verses
Carefully read the two verses cited: 'Allah does what He wills' and 'Allah judges what He wills.' Notice that each uses a different term: 'yasha'' and 'yurid'.
The verses are from Surah Ibrahim and Surah Al-Ma'ida.
2
Identify the author's claim
The author states that for God, irada and mashi'a are one (wahida). This is the foundational claim of the lesson.
3
Reflect on the significance
Why is it important that these are identical for God? Consider that this affirms that God's will is effective and not subject to change.
This distinction becomes clear in the next lesson when we discuss humans.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من الدليل على صفة الإرادة والمشيئة قوله تعالى: ﴿يَفْعَلُ اللهُ مَا يَشَاءُ ﴾ [إبراهيم: ٢٧] . وفي آية أخرى : إِنَّ اللهَ يَحْكُمُ مَايُرِيدُ ﴾ [المائدة: 1] ، وهي والمشيئة واحدة عندنا في حق تعالى. أما في جانب العباد فيفترقان فلو قال رجل لامرأته أردت طلاقكِ لا تطلق، ولو قال لها شئت طلاقكِ يقع به؛ لأن الإرادة مشتقة من الرود وهو الطلب، والمشيئة عبارة عن الإيجاد، فكأنه قال أوجدت طلاقكِ وبه يقع الطلاق كذا ذكروه. أوجدت وقال القونوي : فيه نظر إذ لو كان كذلك لما احتيج إلى النية، والحاصل أن المشيئة عبارة عن الإرادة التامة التي لا يتخلف عنها الفعل، والإرادة تطلق على التامة وعلى غير التامة المرادة في جانب الله تعالى والثانية في جانب العباد انتهى. فالأولى هي وفيه نظر فإنه على هذا كان ينبغي أن يذكر المشيئة في الصفات لا الإرادة.
Then from the proof for the attribute of will and decree is His saying, exalted: 'Allah does what He wills' [Ibrahim: 27]. And in another verse: 'Indeed, Allah judges what He wills' [Al-Ma'ida: 1]. And they (will and decree) are one according to us with respect to the Exalted. But with respect to servants, they differ. For if a man says to his wife 'I intended (aradtu) your divorce,' she is not divorced. But if he says to her 'I willed (shi'tu) your divorce,' it occurs. This is because 'irada' is derived from 'rawd' which means seeking, and 'mashi'a' is expression of bringing into existence. So it is as if he said 'I brought your divorce into existence,' and by it divorce occurs. This is what they mentioned. Al-Qunawi said: there is consideration in this, for if it were so, there would be no need for intention. The summary is that mashi'a is the complete irada from which the action never fails, while irada is used for the complete and for the incomplete intended [will]. In the aspect of God, the first [is meant], and the second is in the aspect of servants. End. So the first is the one. And there is consideration, for according to this, it would be fitting to mention mashi'a in the attributes, not irada.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage provides Quranic proof for the attributes of irada and mashi'a. Two verses are cited: 'Allah does what He wills' and 'Allah judges what He wills'. The author states that in God, irada and mashi'a are identical. However, for humans, they differ. A juristic example is given: if a man says to his wife 'I intended your divorce' (aradtu talaqaki), it does not effect divorce; but if he says 'I willed your divorce' (shi'tu talaqaki), divorce occurs. The reason is that irada comes from 'rawd' meaning seeking, while mashi'a means bringing into existence. So saying 'I willed' is like saying 'I brought your divorce into existence.' Al-Qunawi then comments, offering a refinement: mashi'a is the complete irada that is always followed by the act, while irada can be complete or incomplete. For God, the complete is meant; for humans, the second. Then the author notes a problem: if that distinction is accepted, the original author should have listed mashi'a as an attribute, not irada. This shows an internal critique.

Key Terms
إرادة
مشيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that irada and mashi'a are different attributes of God, just as they are for humans.
Correction: The author explicitly states that they are one (wahida) with respect to God, based on the Quranic usage being interchangeable.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how are irada and mashi'a related to God?
A) They are two different attributes
B) They are one and the same
C) They are opposites
D) They are irrelevant

Key Takeaway
For God, irada and mashi'a are identical; the Quran uses both terms interchangeably to describe His will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God's will is one and undivided, or do you sometimes act as if you can separate His decree from His command? When you sin, do you excuse yourself by saying 'God willed it' while ignoring His command? How will you reconcile your daily choices with the reality that God's will is all-encompassing and singular? Commit to one area where you will submit your will to His command this week.
?
If irada and mashi'a are one in God, then there is no gap between what He wills and what occurs. Are you at peace with every outcome in your life, trusting that it is exactly what God willed? Or do you resent some of His decrees? How can you train your heart to accept this? Write down one decree you struggle with and resolve to recite 'Allah does what He wills' daily.
?
The lesson says 'they are one according to us.' Is your belief merely verbal, or does it transform your reliance on God? Do you actively surrender your plans to His single will? What practical change can you make today to live out this truth?

Distinguishing Irada and Mashi'a in Humans: Divorce Example and Scholarly Refinement
Distinguishes irada and mashi'a in humans via divorce example and scholarly comments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how irada and mashi'a differ in human context, using the divorce example and the scholarly commentary of Al-Qunawi.

Core Idea
In humans, 'irada' implies intention without action, while 'mashi'a' implies bringing about the action.

Having established that the two are identical for God, the author now explains their distinction for human beings. A legal example from divorce illustrates the practical difference, followed by a refinement from Al-Qunawi and a critical note from the author himself. This lesson walks through that discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the divorce example
Read carefully the scenario: a man says 'I intended your divorce' vs 'I willed your divorce.' Note the different outcomes.
The example is from Islamic jurisprudence on the effect of language.
2
Understand the linguistic derivation
The author explains why the sentences have different effects: irada comes from 'rawd' meaning seeking, while mashi'a means bringing into existence (ijad). Thus 'willed' is like saying 'I brought about your divorce.'
3
Analyze Al-Qunawi's refinement
Al-Qunawi objects that if mashi'a were simply bringing into existence, intention would be unnecessary. He refines: mashi'a is the complete irada that always leads to action; irada can be complete or incomplete.
This is a nuanced theological and linguistic point.
4
Consider the author's critique
The author points out a problem: if Al-Qunawi's distinction is correct, then the original author should have listed mashi'a as an attribute, not irada. This shows internal scholarly debate.
Reflect on how such critiques help refine theological understanding.

The author states that in God, irada and mashi'a are identical. However, for humans, they differ. Will and Wish: Divine Unity and Human Distinction
Quranic proof and distinction between irada and mashiya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Quranic proof for the attributes irada and mashiya, their identity in God, and their practical difference in human legal contexts.

Core Idea
In God, will and wish are one; in humans, they differ in legal effect.

This lesson examines a key passage from the Manah al-Rawd al-Azhar that provides Quranic evidence for the divine attributes of will (irada) and wish (mashiya). While these terms refer to the same reality in God, the passage highlights a critical legal distinction when applied to human actions, using the example of divorce. A scholarly debate with al-Qunawi adds depth to the discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic proof
Start with the two verses cited: 'God does what He wishes' and 'God judges what He wills'. Recognize that both attributes are affirmed for God.
2
Understand the unity in God
Note the statement: 'They are one with us concerning the Exalted.' This establishes that irada and mashiya are identical in God.
3
Examine the human distinction
Read the divorce example: saying 'I intended your divorce' does not effect divorce, but 'I wished your divorce' does. The passage explains why: mashiya implies bringing into existence (ijad).
This is a key legal application.
4
Consider al-Qunawi's critique
Al-Qunawi objects: if mashiya always entails execution, why is intention needed? Then he defines mashiya as the complete will that necessarily produces action.
This shows scholarly refinement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من الدليل على صفة الإرادة والمشيئة قوله تعالى: ﴿يَفْعَلُ اللَّهُ مَا يَشَاءُ﴾ [إبراهيم: 27] وفي آية أخرى: ﴿إِنَّ اللَّهَ يَحْكُمُ مَا يُرِيدُ﴾ [المائدة: 1]، وهي والمشيئة واحدة عندنا في حق تعالى. أما في جانب العباد فيفترقان فلو قال رجل لامرأته أردت طلاقكِ لا تطلق، ولو قال لها شئت طلاقكِ يقع به؛ لأن الإرادة مشتقة من الرود وهو الطلب، والمشيئة عبارة عن الإيجاد، فكأنه قال أوجدت طلاقكِ وبه يقع الطلاق كذا ذكروه. وقال القونوي: فيه نظر إذ لو كان كذلك لما احتيج إلى النية، والحاصل أن المشيئة عبارة عن الإرادة التامة التي لا يتخلف عنها الفعل، والإرادة تطلق على التامة وعلى غير التامة المرادة في جانب الله تعالى والثانية في جانب العباد انتهى. فالأولى هي وفيه نظر فإنه على هذا كان ينبغي أن يذكر المشيئة في الصفات لا الإرادة.
Then among the proofs for the attribute of will (irada) and wish (mashiya) is His saying: 'God does what He wishes' (Quran 14:27). And in another verse: 'Indeed, God judges what He wills' (Quran 5:1). They (irada and mashiya) are one with us concerning the Exalted. As for the side of servants, they differ. For if a man says to his wife 'I intended your divorce' (aradtu talaqaki), she is not divorced. But if he says to her 'I wished your divorce' (shi'tu talaqaki), it occurs. Because irada is derived from 'rawd' which is demand, and mashiya is an expression for bringing into existence (ijad). So it is as if he said 'I brought your divorce into existence' and by it divorce occurs; thus they mentioned it. And al-Qunawi said: There is consideration in this, for if it were so, then there would be no need for intention. The conclusion is that mashiya is an expression for the complete will (irada tamma) from which the act does not lag, and irada is applied to complete and to incomplete willed acts on the side of God, and the second on the side of servants. End. The first is it, and there is consideration in it, for upon this, it would have been appropriate to mention mashiya among the attributes, not irada.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage provides Quranic proof for the attributes of irada (will) and mashiya (wish) and then elaborates on their distinction in God versus humans. The author cites two verses: 'God does what He wishes' (14:27) and 'God judges what He wills' (5:1), indicating that both terms refer to the same divine attribute. However, for humans, they differ. He gives a legal example: if a man says to his wife 'I intended your divorce' (aradtu talaqaki), it does not effect divorce because irada here is mere intention. But if he says 'I wished your divorce' (shi'tu talaqaki), divorce occurs because mashiya implies actual bringing into existence (ijad). The author explains that irada derives from 'rawd' meaning demand, while mashiya signifies creation. Al-Qunawi then comments that this distinction is problematic because if mashiya always entails execution, intention would be unnecessary. His conclusion is that mashiya is the complete will from which the act necessarily follows, whereas irada is more general, covering both complete and incomplete will. For God, both are perfect; for humans, irada can be incomplete. There follows a critical observation: if the standard is that mashiya is the complete will, then it would have been more appropriate to list mashiya among the divine attributes rather than irada. This reflects an internal debate among scholars about which term is primary.

Key Terms
إرادة
مشيئة
إيجاد
الرود
الطلاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming irada and mashiya are always interchangeable in all contexts.
Correction: In God they are one, but in human legal contexts they differ: irada (intention) does not necessarily effect action, while mashiya (wish as bringing into existence) does.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does saying 'shi'tu talaqaki' effect divorce while 'aradtu talaqaki' does not?
A) Because 'shi'tu' is stronger than 'aradtu'
B) Because mashiya is derived from a root meaning 'demand' and irada from 'existence'
C) Because mashiya is an expression for bringing into existence (ijad), while irada is derived from 'rawd' meaning demand
D) Because the Quran uses only 'mashiya' for divorce

Key Takeaway
Irada and mashiya are identical in God, but in humans mashiya implies effective execution while irada may remain mere intention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'if Allah wills' (insha'Allah) about your plans, is it a genuine surrender to His complete will (mashiya) or a casual phrase that hides a lack of commitment? How does the distinction between irada and mashiya in this lesson call you to examine the level of tawakkul (reliance) in your daily speech?
?
Al-Qunawi questioned the need for intention if mashiya always produces the act. In your own life, are there intentions you hold that never become actions? What does that reveal about the gap between your irada and the tawfiq (divine facilitation) needed to turn intention into reality?
?
The author ends by suggesting that mashiya should have been listed among God's attributes instead of irada. Does your personal understanding of Allah's will lean more towards irada (demand/desire) or mashiya (effective execution)? How might this shift your perception of His power and your own accountability?

Answering the Objection on Pharaoh and Abu Jahl
Responds to an objection regarding God's command vs. decree.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the distinction between two types of divine request (command vs. decree) resolves the objection that God's command should necessitate obedience.

Core Idea
God's command leaves room for human choice; His decree always brings existence.

This passage addresses a theological objection: if God's will is all-powerful, why did Pharaoh and Abu Jahl not believe despite being commanded? The author distinguishes two kinds of divine request – one that respects human choice (command) and one that is unconditionally creative (will) – to show that God's command does not compel belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the opening 'If it is said...' part. Understand the objector's premise: if irada and mashiya are identical, then God's command to believe should result in belief, just as His will brings things into existence.
The objector assumes command and will are the same kind of request.
2
Identify the two types of divine request
In the answer, find the two categories: (1) a request based on the servant's choice (called amr/command), and (2) a request not based on choice (called mashiya/irada). Note how each is defined.
The first type does not entail existence; the second does.
3
Understand why command does not necessitate belief
Reflect on why command (amr) does not force obedience: it is tied to the servant's free choice (ikhtiyar). The servant may choose to disobey. This explains the disbelief of Pharaoh and Abu Jahl.
This preserves human responsibility.
4
Note the consequence for the second type
For the second type (mashiya/irada), existence necessarily follows. If it didn't, that would imply inability (ajz) in God, which is impossible. This shows God's perfection.
God is free from incapacity.
5
Apply to the objection
See how the distinction resolves the problem: the command to believe is of the first type, so lack of belief does not conflict with God's power or the identity of irada and mashiya.
The objection mistakenly conflated the two types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: إن الله تعالى طلب الإيمان من فرعون وأبي جهل وأمثالهما بالأمر ولم يوجد منهم الإيمان، فلو كانت الإرادة والمشيئة واحدة كما زعمتم لوجد ذلك منهم؛ لأن المشيئة هي الإيجاد. قلنا: الطلب من الله تعالى على نوعين: طلب من المكلف على وجه الاختيار وهو المسمى بالأمر ولا يلزم منه الوجود لتعلقه باختيار المكلف. وطلب لا تعلق له باختيار المكلف المسمى بالمشيئة والإرادة والوجود من لوازمهما، إذ لو لم يكن يلزم العجز، وهو سبحانه وتعالى منزه عنه بخلاف العباد.
If it is said: 'Verily God Almighty requested faith from Pharaoh, Abu Jahl, and their likes by command, and faith did not occur from them. So if irada and mashiya were one as you claim, that would have occurred from them, because mashiya is bringing into existence.' We say: Request from God Almighty is of two types: A request from the morally responsible (mukallaf) on the basis of choice, which is called command (amr), and existence does not necessarily follow from it because it is connected to the choice of the mukallaf. And a request not connected to the choice of the mukallaf, called mashiya and irada, and existence is a necessary concomitant of them, since if it did not necessarily follow, it would entail inability, and He, glorified and exalted, is transcendent above that, unlike the servants.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (fayl qīl) that addresses a theological problem. The objector argues: If divine irada and mashiya are identical as claimed, then when God commanded Pharaoh and Abu Jahl to believe (using the term 'amr'), belief should have necessarily occurred from them, since mashiya implies actual creation. Since they did not believe, the identity claim fails. The answer distinguishes two types of divine request (talab): (1) A request addressed to the mukallaf (accountable person) that is based on choice (ikhtiyar), called command (amr). This does not entail actual occurrence because the servant's free choice is involved; the servant may disobey. (2) A request that is not connected to the servant's choice, called mashiya and irada, and this necessarily entails existence. If it did not, God would be incapable (ajz), which is impossible for God. Thus, the command to believe is of the first type, so lack of belief does not contradict the identity of irada and mashiya in God. The author concludes that God is transcendent above any inability, whereas creatures are subject to it.

Key Terms
طلب
الأمر
اختيار
عجز
مشيئة
إرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's command to believe must always result in belief, otherwise God's power is limited.
Correction: God's command (amr) is a request that respects human choice, so it does not compel obedience. His will (mashiya) is what brings about existence unconditionally. Thus, disbelief under command does not limit God's power.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the objection that Pharaoh and Abu Jahl did not believe despite God's command?
A) God did not truly want them to believe
B) The command (amr) is a request that leaves room for the servant's choice, so belief does not necessarily follow
C) Their disbelief was due to a weakness in God's power
D) The verses about their disbelief are metaphorical

Key Takeaway
God's command is a request that respects human choice, while His will (mashiya) is creative and unconditional, preserving both divine power and human responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fail to obey a clear command of God, do you secretly blame God for not making you obey, or do you take full responsibility for your choice? How does this lesson's distinction between command and will expose the excuses you hide behind?
?
If you truly believed that every divine command is a genuine request that respects your free will, would you treat His commands as serious personal calls or as mere suggestions you can ignore? What specific command are you neglecting today because you assume it doesn't really matter?
?
How often do you expect God to simply make you do good rather than striving with your own choice? What would change in your daily life if you acted on the truth that God's command leaves the outcome up to you, and you alone will be held accountable?

The Eternality of Divine Wisdom (Hikma)
States that wisdom (hikma) is an eternal attribute, contrary to al-Ash'ari's distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that in Maturidi theology, the attribute of wisdom (hikma) is eternal whether it refers to knowledge or precise action, and how this differs from al-Ash'ari's position.

Core Idea
Divine wisdom is eternal in all its meanings — both knowledge and action.

This lesson delves into the attribute of wisdom (hikma) and its eternal nature. The author clarifies that regardless of how we interpret wisdom — as knowledge or as precise action — it remains an eternal attribute of Allah, contrary to the Ash'ari view that only the knowledge aspect is eternal. The passage also includes a cryptic remark by al-Qunawi linking qadr to missing knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two meanings of hikma
Read the passage and note that wisdom can mean either knowledge (ilm) or precision of action (ihkam al-amal).
The Arabic word hikma has a broad semantic range.
2
Compare Maturidi and Ash'ari views
Identify the difference: Maturidis say hikma is always eternal; Ash'aris say only if it means knowledge.
This distinction is a key point of sectarian divergence.
3
Reflect on al-Qunawi's statement
Consider what 'al-Qadr is the missing knowledge' might imply about human understanding of divine decree.
This statement is allusive and requires careful interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الحكمة سواء كانت بمعنى العلم أو إحكام العمل، فصفة أزلية عندنا خلافا للأشعري حيث قال: إن أريد بها العلم فهي أزلية، وإن أريد بها الفعل فلا إذ التكوين حادث عنده، قال القونوي: «القدر هو العلم المفقود».
Then wisdom (hikma), whether it is in the sense of knowledge (ilm) or precision of action (ihkam al-amal), is an eternal attribute (sifa azaliyya) with us, contrary to al-Ash'ari, for he said: If what is meant by it is knowledge, then it is eternal; but if what is meant by it is action, then it is not, because takwin (bringing into existence) is temporal (hadith) according to him. Al-Qunawi said: 'al-Qadr (decreed measure) is the missing knowledge.'
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a concise doctrinal statement on the attribute of wisdom (hikma). The author asserts that according to his school (the Maturidi view), hikma is an eternal attribute regardless of interpretation: whether it means divine knowledge (ilm) or the precise execution of actions (ihkam al-amal). This differs from al-Ash'ari, who distinguishes: if hikma means knowledge, it is eternal; but if it means the action of bringing things into existence (takwin), it is temporal (hadith) because he holds that takwin is a created act. The author then quotes al-Qunawi's cryptic remark: 'al-Qadr (the decreed measure) is the missing knowledge.' This likely alludes to the idea that qadr (predestination) is itself a form of knowledge that humans lack. The passage thus reaffirms the Maturidi position that God's attribute of wisdom is eternal in all respects, countering the Ash'ari position that some aspects (like creation) are temporal.

Key Terms
حكمة
علم
إحكام العمل
أزلية
تكوين
قدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that wisdom only refers to knowledge, and that only knowledge is eternal.
Correction: The passage clarifies that wisdom includes both knowledge and precise action, and both are eternal attributes in the Maturidi view.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the status of wisdom (hikma) as a divine attribute?
A) It is eternal only if it means knowledge
B) It is temporal if it means action
C) It is eternal whether it means knowledge or precision of action
D) It is not an attribute of God at all

Key Takeaway
In the Maturidi school, divine wisdom is eternal in both senses, affirming God's perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that God's wisdom is perfect and eternal. But when life brings trials you cannot understand, do you truly trust that His wisdom encompasses both His knowledge and the precise execution of His actions? Or do you doubt that His decree has a hidden wisdom? How will you actively remind yourself of this eternal attribute in your next moment of confusion?
?
Al-Ash'ari separated God's act of bringing into existence from His eternal wisdom. In your own life, are there commands of God that you obey mechanically, without the deep conviction that they flow from His timeless wisdom? What specific act of worship or obedience will you transform this week by consciously linking it to the belief that God's wisdom is eternal and perfect in both knowledge and action?
?
Al-Qunawi said that qadr (divine decree) is 'the missing knowledge'—a wisdom that humans lack. When you struggle to accept God's decree, are you effectively claiming that you know better than His eternal wisdom? What will you do today to surrender that false sense of knowledge and humbly submit to His decree as a manifestation of His all-encompassing wisdom?

Two Types of Divine Will in the Quran
Classifying divine will into qadariyya and diniyya with Quranic proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between God's cosmic will (irada qadariyya) and His religious will (irada diniyya) as taught by Ahl al-Sunna, and why the divine command entails only the religious will.

Core Idea
God's will has two aspects: one creates everything, the other commands what He loves.

The Quran speaks of God's will in different contexts. The scholars of Ahl al-Sunna have clarified that there are two distinct types of divine will: a universal will that encompasses all events, and a specific will that reflects God's command and pleasure. This passage presents this classification with clear Quranic evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's classification come through.
Notice the two types are named with multiple adjectives.
2
Identify the two types of irada
Find the names of the first and second type. Note that the first is called qadariyya, kawniyya, khalqiyya; the second is diniyya, amriyya, shar'iyya.
The first is associated with mashiya (wish), the second with love and pleasure.
3
Examine the Quranic evidence
Look at the two verses cited. The first (6:125) shows God's will for guidance and misguidance — that is cosmic will. The second (2:185) shows God willing ease — that is religious will.
4
Understand the conclusion about command
The passage ends by stating that the divine command (amr) entails the second will, not the first. Reflect on why this distinction matters: God can command something without necessarily decreeing its occurrence.
This solves the problem of why some disobey despite God's command.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا، والمحققون من أهل السنة يقولون: الإرادة في كتاب الله تعالى نوعان: الأولى: إرادة قدرية كونية خلقية وهي المشيئة الشاملة لجميع الحوادث، لقوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَنْ يُرِدِ اللَّهُ أَنْ يَهْدِيَهُ يَشْرَحْ صَدْرَهُ لِلْإِسْلَامِ وَمَنْ يُرِدْ أَنْ يُضِلَّهُ يَجْعَلْ صَدْرَهُ ضَيِّقًا حَرَجًا كَأَنَّمَا يَصَّعَّدُ فِي السَّمَاءِ﴾ [الأنعام: 125]. والثانية: إرادة دينية أمرية شرعية وهي المتضمنة للمحبة والرضى كقوله تعالى: ﴿يُرِيدُ اللَّهُ بِكُمُ الْيُسْرَ وَلَا يُرِيدُ بِكُمُ الْعُسْرَ﴾ [البقرة: 185] وأمثال ذلك، والأمر يستلزم الإرادة الثانية دون الأولى.
This, and the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) from Ahl al-Sunna say: Will (irada) in the Book of God Almighty is of two types: The first: qadariyya (decreetal), kawniyya (cosmic), khalqiyya (creative) will, which is mashiya (wish) encompassing all events, due to His saying: 'So whoever God intends (yurid) to guide, He expands his breast to Islam; and whoever He intends to misguide, He makes his breast tight and constricted as if he were climbing up into the sky' (Quran 6:125). The second: diniyya (religious), amriyya (commanding), shar'iyya (legislative) will, which includes love and satisfaction, like His saying: 'God intends (yurid) ease for you and does not intend hardship for you' (Quran 2:185) and similar verses. And the command (amr) entails the second will, not the first.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a classification of divine will as found in the Quran, presented as the view of the 'verifiers' (muhaqqiqun) among Ahl al-Sunna. Two types are distinguished: (1) Qadariyya, kawniyya, khalqiyya will: This is the cosmic, creative will that encompasses all events without exception. It is identical to mashiya (wish) and applies to everything that occurs, including guidance and misguidance. The author cites Quran 6:125, where 'yurid' (He intends) regarding guidance and misguidance is clearly a reference to this all-encompassing will. (2) Diniyya, amriyya, shar'iyya will: This is the religious, legislative will that is tied to God's love and pleasure. It does not necessarily entail actual creation; rather, it expresses what God commands and loves. The example is Quran 2:185, where God intends ease, not hardship. The author then draws a key conclusion: the divine command (amr) entails the second type of will (irada diniyya), not the first. This means that when God commands something, He wills it in the sense of loving and approving it, but He may not decree its actual occurrence in the cosmic sense. This distinction solves the problem of why God commands belief but some do not believe: the religious will is fulfilled in the command, not necessarily in the event.

Key Terms
إرادة قدرية
إرادة دينية
مشيئة
أمر
كونية
شرعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that when God commands something, He necessarily wills its occurrence in the cosmic sense.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the divine command only entails the religious will (irada diniyya), not the cosmic will. God may command something without decreeing it to happen.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the divine command (amr) entail?
A) The cosmic will (irada qadariyya)
B) The religious will (irada diniyya) only
C) Both types of will equally
D) Neither type of will

Key Takeaway
God's will is of two kinds: cosmic (universal) and religious (commanding), and His commands are always in line with the religious will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a command from Allah, do you assume that since He commanded it, He will also cause you to do it? How does this distinction between cosmic and religious will challenge your view of personal responsibility?
?
Consider a sin you committed. Did you ever say 'Allah willed it' as an excuse? How does the distinction between cosmic and religious will affect how you view your own choices and accountability?
?
What does it mean for your worship that Allah's commands are an expression of His love, even when you struggle to fulfill them? How should that change your attitude toward obedience and draw you nearer to Him?

Two Types of Divine Will: Qadariyyah and Diniyyah
Distinguishes two types of divine will and resolves a scholarly dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between Allah's creational will (iradah qadariyyah) and His religious will (iradah diniyyah), and how this resolves the tension between His universal will and His command against sin.

Core Idea
Divine will has two dimensions: one creates all, the other commands what He loves.

The scholars of Ahl al-Sunnah faced a delicate question: if everything that happens is by Allah's will, does He will evil and disbelief? Some avoided detailed statements, while others carefully qualified them. This passage presents the mature resolution: Allah has two distinct types of will, one universal and creative, the other legislative and beloved. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping the Sunni position on divine decree and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the scholarly dispute
Begin by reading the passage from 'Then the expressions of our companions...' up to '...this is the choice of al-Maturidi.' Notice two different approaches: one avoids detailed attribution of evil to Allah, the other qualifies it.
The passage first reports disagreement before giving the resolution.
2
Identify the two types of will
Read the resolution section from 'the investigators from Ahl al-Sunnah say' onward. Identify the two types of will: iradah qadariyyah and iradah diniyyah. Note their names and descriptions.
Iradah qadariyyah is also called mashiah.
3
Examine the Quranic proofs
Look at the two Quranic verses quoted. The first (6:125) illustrates the universal inclusive will; the second (2:185) illustrates the legislative will that includes divine pleasure.
Notice that the first verse includes both guidance and misguidance.
4
Understand the conclusion
Read the final sentence: 'a command entails the second will but not the first.' Reflect on how this solves the problem: Allah commands good but does not will it in the creational sense for everyone.
This is the key theological resolution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اختلفت عبارات أصحابنا رحمهم الله فى هذه المسئلة قال بعضهم: نقول: إن جميع الموجودات والأفعال مراد الله تعالى ولا نقول على التفصيل: إن القبائح والشرور والمعاصي لله، كما نقول على الإجمال: إنه خالق لجميع الموجودات، ولا نقول على التفصيل: إنه خالق الجيف والقاذورات. وقال بعضهم: نقول على التفصيل ولكن مقروناً بقرينة تليق به، فنقول: إنه أراد الكفر من الكافر كسبا له شراً قبيحاً منهيًا عنه، كما أراد الإيمان من المؤمن كسبا له خيرا حسنا مأمورا به فهو اختيار الماتريدي، وبه قال الأشعري. هذا، والمحققون من أهل السنة يقولون: الإرادة في كتاب الله تعالى نوعان: الأولى: إرادة قدرية كونية خلقية وهي المشيئة الشاملة لجميع الحوادث، لقوله تعالى: فَمَنْ يُرِدِ اللَّهُ أَنْ يَهْدِيَهُ يَشْرَحْ صَدْرَهُ لِلْإِسْلَامِ وَمَنْ يُرِدْ أَنْ يُضِلَّهُ يَجْعَلْ صَدْرَهُ ضَيِّقًا حَرَجًا كَأَنَّمَا يَصَّعَّدُ فِي السَّمَاءِ [الأنعام: 125] . والثانية: إرادة دينية أمرية شرعية وهي المتضمنة للمحبة والرضى كقوله تعالى: يُرِيدُ اللَّهُ بِكُمُ الْيُسْرَ وَلَا يُرِيدُ بِكُمُ الْعُسْرَ [البقرة:185] وأمثال ذلك، والأمر يستلزم الإرادة الثانية دون الأولى.
Then the expressions of our companions (may Allah have mercy on them) differed in this issue. Some of them said: We say that all existents and actions are willed by Allah, but we do not say in detail that ugly things, evils, and sins are from Allah, just as we say in summary that He is the Creator of all existents, but we do not say in detail that He is the Creator of carcasses and filth. And some of them said: We say in detail but accompanied by a fitting qualification, so we say: He willed disbelief from the disbeliever as an acquisition for him, being evil, ugly, and prohibited, just as He willed faith from the believer as an acquisition for him, being good, beautiful, and commanded. This is the choice of al-Maturidi, and al-Ash'ari also said it. Now, the investigators from Ahl al-Sunnah say: Will (iradah) in the Book of Allah is of two types: First: creational, cosmic, creative will (iradah qadariyyah kawniyyah khilqiyyah), which is the mashiah encompassing all events, as He says: 'So whoever Allah wants to guide, He expands his breast to Islam, and whoever He wants to mislead, He makes his breast tight and constricted, as if he were climbing into the sky' (al-An'am, 125). Second: religious, command-based, legislative will (iradah diniyyah amriyyah shar'iyyah), which includes love and pleasure, as He says: 'Allah wants ease for you and does not want hardship for you' (al-Baqarah, 185) and similar verses. And a command (al-amr) entails the second will but not the first.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage begins by reporting a difference among Hanafi scholars on how to attribute actions to Allah. Some scholars prefer to state only in general that all things are willed by Allah, avoiding detailed specification of evil and ugly things, similar to how they avoid saying He creates carcasses and filth. Other scholars, however, allow detailed specification but always accompany it with a qualification that shows the action is evil as a kasb (acquisition) for the servant. The author notes that al-Maturidi and al-Ash'ari agreed on this second approach. Then the passage presents the resolution by the muhaqqiqun (investigators) of Ahl al-Sunnah: they distinguish two types of divine will. The first is iradah qadariyyah (creational will), also called mashiah, which encompasses all events universally, whether good or evil. Evidence is given from Quran 6:125, where Allah's will to guide or mislead results in physical effects. The second is iradah diniyyah (religious will), which is specifically related to commands and includes divine love and pleasure. Evidence is given from Quran 2:185, where Allah wills ease. The passage concludes that a divine command (al-amr) only entails the second type of will, not the first, thus resolving the apparent tension between God's universal will and His command against sins.

Key Terms
إرادة قدرية
إرادة دينية
مشيئة
الأمر
كسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If Allah wills (in the creational sense) something, He must love and approve of it.
Correction: The passage clarifies that Allah's creational will (iradah qadariyyah) includes all events, but His legislative will (iradah diniyyah) alone carries His love and pleasure. He can will something in the first sense without willing it in the second, as with disbelief and sin.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between the two types of divine will?
A) One is for believers, one for disbelievers.
B) One is creational and inclusive, the other is legislative and includes love and pleasure.
C) One is in the Qur'an, the other in the Hadith.
D) One is temporal, the other eternal.

Key Takeaway
Allah's universal, creational will (mashiah) covers everything, but His command-based will (iradah diniyyah) only covers what He loves and commands, resolving the paradox of divine will and human sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you find yourself passively attributing it to Allah's will, or do you actively acknowledge your own responsibility as the one who acquired that sin? How does recognizing the two types of will push you to repent rather than resign?
?
Has there been a moment when you desired something that was clearly against Allah's command? How does the truth that Allah's religious will aims for your ease and goodness change your response to such desires?
?
Are you willing to accept that Allah's creational will includes events that seem unjust or evil, while trusting that His religious will never commands anything but what is good? Does this distinction give you peace or raise new struggles in your heart?

Distinguishing al-Azamah and al-Kibriya'
Author lists seven essential attributes and argues distinction between al-azamah and al-kibriya'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between two divine attributes, al-azamah and al-kibriya', and why they are not synonymous.

Core Idea
Al-Kibriya' and al-Azamah are as distinct as a cloak and a garment.

This lesson examines the subtle distinction between two closely related divine attributes. The author, building on Imam Abu Hanifa's enumeration of essential attributes, focuses on al-azamah and al-kibriya'. Through a hadith qudsi and scholarly commentary, we learn that despite their close meanings, they are fundamentally different.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the listing of seven attributes and the focus on the last two.
2
Understand the seven essential attributes
Identify the seven attributes mentioned: al-ahadiyyah, al-wahidiyyah, al-samadiyyah, al-azamah, al-kibriya'. Recognize that the author focuses on the latter two.
These are from Imam Abu Hanifa's list.
3
Examine the definitions by opposites
See how al-Baydawi defines al-azam and al-kabir by their opposites (haqir, saghir). The author adds al-'ali opposite al-dani.
Opposites help clarify meaning.
4
Analyze the proof from hadith qudsi
The author quotes a hadith qudsi where Allah says: 'Magnificence (al-kibriya') is My cloak and Majesty (al-azamah) is My garment.' Understand how the distinction between a cloak and a garment shows they are not synonymous.
The cloak is nobler than the garment.
5
Reflect on the practical implication
The author notes that the key phrase of prayer is 'Allahu Akbar' (using kabir), not 'Allahu A'zam', indicating the primacy of al-kibriya' in this context.
Worship reflects theological distinctions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالإمام الأعظم رحمه الله ذكر هذه السبعة من الصفات الذاتية، ومنها الأحدية في الذات والواحدية في الصفات والصمدية المستغنية عن الممكنات والعظمة والكبرياء على ما ورد في الأسماء والصفات. قال البيضاوي: «العظم نقيض الحقير، والكبير نقيض الصغير». أقول: والعلي نقيض الدني، فهذه ألفاظ متقاربة المعنى في الأسماء الحسنى، والقول بأنها ألفاظ مترادفة صدر عن أحوال متكاثفة، فقد قال حجة الإسلام ينبغي أن نعتقد تفاوتا بين معنى اللفظين فإنه يصعب علينا وجه الفرق بين معنييه في حق الله تعالى ولكنا مع ذلك لا نشك في أصل الافتراق ولذلك قال الله تعالى: «الكبرياء ردائي والعظمة إزاري». ففرق بينهما فرقا يدل على التفاوت، فإن كلا من الرداء والإزار زينة للإنسان، ولكن الرداء أشرف من الإزار ولذا جعل مفتاح الصلاة لفظ الله أكبر.
The Greatest Imam (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned these seven from the essential attributes (al-sifat al-dhatiyyah). Among them are: al-ahadiyyah (uniqueness) in essence, al-wahidiyyah (oneness) in attributes, al-samadiyyah (eternal independence) which is independence from contingents, al-azamah (majesty), and al-kibriya' (greatness) according to what has been transmitted in the names and attributes. Al-Baydawi said: 'al-azam is the opposite of al-haqir (insignificant), al-kabir is the opposite of al-saghir (small).' I say: al-'ali (the High) is the opposite of al-dani (low). So these are words with close meanings among the beautiful names. The statement that they are synonymous words arises from compacted states. Indeed, Hujjat al-Islam (al-Ghazali) said: It is necessary to believe that there is a difference between the meaning of the two words, even if it is difficult for us to find the exact distinction between their two meanings with respect to Allah the Exalted. However, despite that, we do not doubt that they are fundamentally distinct. That is why Allah the Exalted said: 'Magnificence (al-kibriya') is My cloak and Majesty (al-azamah) is My garment.' So He differentiated between them in a way that indicates disparity, for each of the cloak and the garment is an adornment for a human, but the cloak is nobler than the garment, and for this reason the key to prayer is made the phrase 'Allahu Akbar' (Allah is the Greatest).
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage first lists the seven essential attributes mentioned by Imam Abu Hanifa (al-Imam al-A'zam). These include al-ahadiyyah (uniqueness in essence), al-wahidiyyah (oneness in attributes), al-samadiyyah (eternal independence from all contingents), al-azamah (majesty), and al-kibriya' (greatness), as known from the names and attributes literature. Then the author discusses the two specific attributes al-azamah and al-kibriya'. He quotes al-Baydawi defining each by their opposites: al-azam is the opposite of insignificant, al-kabir is the opposite of small. He adds that al-'ali (the High) is opposite of al-dani (low). He notes that these words have close meanings among the beautiful names, so some might consider them synonyms. However, he attributes the claim of synonymity to 'compacted states' and cites al-Ghazali (Hujjat al-Islam) who insists that we must believe in a real difference between them, even if we cannot articulate it fully. The author proves this difference from a hadith qudsi where Allah says: 'Magnificence (al-kibriya') is My cloak and Majesty (al-azamah) is My garment.' The distinction between a cloak (rida') and a garment (izar) shows that they are not identical; the cloak is nobler. This is why the key phrase of prayer is 'Allahu Akbar' (Allah is the Greatest), not 'Allahu A'zam', indicating the primacy of al-kibriya' over al-azamah in this context.

الأحدية

العظم
الكبير

الإزار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that al-azamah and al-kibriya' are exact synonyms.
Correction: The passage shows they are distinct, as Allah Himself distinguishes them as cloak and garment in a hadith qudsi.

Checkpoint
What proof does the author give that al-azamah and al-kibriya' are not synonymous?
A) They have different opposites.
B) The hadith qudsi differentiates between cloak and garment.
C) Al-Ghazali said so.
D) They are used in different contexts in the Qur'an.

Key Takeaway
Al-kibriya' and al-azamah are distinct attributes, as proven by the hadith qudsi, and this distinction has practical implications in worship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allahu Akbar' in your prayer, do you consciously reflect on the attribute of al-kibriya' that Allah has reserved as His cloak? How can you deepen that awareness starting today?
?
The passage teaches that even when we cannot fully grasp the distinction between Allah's attributes, we must affirm them. Are there any aspects of Allah's names that you have overlooked or treated as unimportant? Commit to learning one new name this week and reflecting on its meaning.
?
If the cloak of al-kibriya' is nobler than the garment of al-azamah, how should that affect the awe you feel when you pronounce 'Allahu Akbar'? What specific change will you make in your next prayer to manifest that awe?

Is Baqa' a Positive or Negative Attribute?
The author explains the disagreement on baqa' and argues for its negative nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the more apparent view considers al-baqa' (permanence) as a negative attribute rather than an additional positive attribute.

Core Idea
Baqa' negates non-existence and annihilation; it is not a positive addition.

In this passage, the author builds on the previous discussion of the seven positive essential attributes and addresses a disagreement about baqa'. While some authorities include baqa' as an eighth positive attribute, the stronger position is that it is a negative attribute. Let's explore the reasoning behind this.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage from 'فهذه السبعة' to 'ممتنع قدمه' and its translation. Note the two opinions mentioned.
The couplet lists the positive attributes including baqa'.
2
Identify the two views
The first view considers baqa' as affirmative (positive). The second, preferred view sees it as negative. Find the reasoning for each.
3
Understand the logical principle
The author states that baqa' negates previous non-existence and later annihilation, based on the principle: what is eternal cannot cease, and what can cease cannot be eternal.
This principle supports the negative interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذه السبعة هي الصفات الذاتية الثبوتية واختلف في البقاء أنه من النعوت السلبية؟ فبنى على الأول بعضهم وجمعها في بيت فقال: من حياة وعلم قدرة وإرادة كلام وإبصار وسمع مع البقا. والأظهر أنه من النعوت السلبية، فإن المراد به نفي العدم السابق والفناء اللاحق بناء على أن ما ثبت قدمه استحال عدمه وما يجوز عدمه ممتنع قدمه. وأما ما وقع في متن لمولانا عمر النسفي من قوله: الحي القادر العليم السميع البصير الشائي المريد، فقد يوهم أن المشيئة والإرادة متغايران وليس كذلك لما سبق الكلام على هذا المقام.
These seven are the positive essential attributes (al-sifat al-dhatiyyah al-thubutiyyah). And there is disagreement regarding al-baqa' (permanence): is it among the negative attributes (al-nu'ut al-salbiyyah)? Some built upon the first view and collected them in a couplet: 'From life, knowledge, power, will, speech, sight, hearing, with permanence.' And the more apparent view is that it is from the negative attributes, because what is intended by it is the negation of previous non-existence and subsequent annihilation, based on the principle that whatever has been established as eternal is impossible to cease, and whatever is possible to cease is impossible to be eternal. As for what occurred in the text of Mawlana 'Umar al-Nasafi, where he said: 'al-Hayy, al-Qadir, al-'Alim, al-Sami', al-Basir, al-Sha'i, al-Murid' – this might give the impression that al-mashiah and al-iradah are two different things, but they are not, as the discussion on this topic has preceded.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage begins by summarizing the seven attributes just discussed as positive essential attributes (al-sifat al-dhatiyyah al-thubutiyyah). It then raises a point of disagreement: whether al-baqa' (permanence) is among the negative attributes (al-nu'ut al-salbiyyah). Some scholars take the first view (i.e., that the seven are positive) and add baqa' as an eighth positive attribute, expressing this in a couplet that lists life, knowledge, power, will, speech, sight, hearing, and permanence. However, the author states that the more apparent view is that baqa' is a negative attribute. The reason is that baqa' functions to negate two things: previous non-existence (al-'adam al-sabiq) and later annihilation (al-fana' al-lahiq). The logical principle is that whatever is established as eternal (qadim) is impossible to cease, and whatever is possible to cease cannot be eternal. Thus baqa' does not add a positive content but merely denies non-existence. The passage then addresses a potential confusion arising from the text of Mawlana 'Umar al-Nasafi, who in his famous creedal list includes both 'al-Sha'i' (the One who wills by mashiah) and 'al-Murid' (the One who wills by iradah). This might give the impression that mashiah and iradah are two distinct attributes. The author clarifies that they are not distinct; they are synonymous, as discussed earlier in the work.

Key Terms
البقاء
النعوت السلبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think baqa' is an additional positive attribute alongside the seven.
Correction: The more apparent view is that baqa' is a negative attribute; it does not add a positive meaning but rather denies non-existence.

Checkpoint
Why is al-baqa' considered a negative attribute according to the author?
A) Because it is not mentioned in the Qur'an.
B) Because it negates non-existence and annihilation.
C) Because it is not included in al-Nasafi's list.
D) Because it is not an attribute of perfection.

Key Takeaway
Baqa' is a negative attribute; it does not add a positive concept but negates non-existence and annihilation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author argues that baqa' is merely a negation of non-existence, not a positive addition. How does this theological detail challenge your natural tendency to imagine God's attributes as human-like qualities? In what specific way will you adjust your mental image of God when you say 'Allah is Ever-Living'?
?
If God's permanence is only the impossibility of His non-existence, then your entire existence is a gift that could be revoked at any moment. What concrete change in your daily reliance on Allah does this truth demand starting today?
?
Some scholars counted baqa' as an eighth positive attribute. The author disagrees. Does this distinction affect how you feel about God's nearness? Reflect: Does God's self-sufficiency (that He needs no added quality) make you trust Him more or less? How will you act on that trust this week?

Mashiah and Iradah: One Attribute or Two?
The author clarifies that mashiah and iradah are synonymous attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that mashiah (will) and iradah (will) are synonymous attributes in Sunni creed, contrary to what al-Nasafi's enumeration might suggest.

Core Idea
Mashiah and iradah are two names for the same divine attribute.

The author addresses a potential confusion from the text of Mawlana 'Umar al-Nasafi, who lists both 'al-Sha'i' (the Willer) and 'al-Murid' (the Willer). Some might think these refer to distinct attributes, but the author clarifies they are synonymous. This builds on an earlier discussion.

Identify the potential confusion
Al-Nasafi's list includes both terms, which could imply they are different. The author warns against this misunderstanding.
3
Understand the author's clarification
The author states that mashiah and iradah are not different; they are synonymous, as previously discussed in the work.
Recall the earlier discussion that established their synonymy.

beginner

The Seven Essential Attributes and Their Positive Nature
Enumeration of the seven essential attributes and discussion of synonymy among some

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven essential attributes of Allah as listed by Imam Abu Hanifa and why they are classified as positive attributes.

Core Idea
Allah's seven essential attributes are positive, affirming perfection.

Imam Abu Hanifa identified seven essential attributes that describe Allah's essence. In this passage, the author enumerates them and clarifies that they are positive (thubutiyya) attributes, meaning they affirm something real about Allah. We also explore the subtle distinction among attributes like greatness and majesty.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration of attributes
Read the part where Imam Abu Hanifa lists the seven essential attributes, including oneness, self-sufficiency, greatness, and majesty.
Note that the full list of seven is later confirmed explicitly.
2
Understand the synonymy discussion
Notice how the author quotes al-Baydawi and al-Ghazali to argue that even if attributes like 'azm and kabir seem similar, there is a real distinction between them.
The hadith qudsi distinguishes them by using 'cloak' and 'wrapper'.
3
Identify the conclusion
The passage ends by affirming that these seven are the positive essential attributes, setting the stage for the discussion on baqa'.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالإمام الأعظم رحمه الله ذكر هذه السبعة من الصفات الذاتية، ومنها الأحدية في الذات والواحدية في الصفات والصمدية المستغنية عن الممكنات والعظمة والكبرياء على ما ورد في الأسماء والصفات. قال البيضاوي: «العظم نقيض الحقير، والكبير نقيض الصغير». أقول: والعلي نقيض الدني، فهذه الفاظ متقاربة المعنى في الأسماء الحسنى، والقول بأنها ألفاظ مترادفة صدر عن أحوال متكاثفة، فقد قال حجة الإسلام ينبغي أن نعتقد تفاوتا بين معنى اللفظين فإنه يصعب علينا وجه الفرق بين معنييهما في حق الله تعالى ولكنا مع ذلك لا نشك في أصل الافتراق ولذلك قال الله تعالى: ((الكبرياء ردائي والعظمة إزاري)). ففرق بينهما فرقا يدل على التفاوت، فإن كلا من الرداء والإزار زينة للانسان، ولكن الرداء أشرف من الإزار ولذا جعل مفتاح الصلاة لفظ الله أكبر، الصفات الثبوتية أو من النعوت فهذه السبعة هي الصفات الذاتية الثبوتية واختلف في البقاء أنه من الصفات الثبوتية أم السلبية؟ فبنى على الأول بعضهم وجمعها في بيت فقال: من حياة وعلم قدرة وإرادة كلام وإبصار وسمع مع البقا "العقائد" والأظهر أنه من النعوت السلبية، فإن المراد به نفي العدم السابق والفناء اللاحق بناء على أن ما ثبت قدمه استحال عدمه وما يجوز عدمه ممتنع قدمه. وأما ما وقع في متن لمولانا عمر النسفي من قوله : الحي القادر العليم السميع البصير الشائي المريد، فقد يوهم أن المشيئة والإرادة متغايران وليس كذلك لما سبق الكلام على هذا المقام.
The Greatest Imam, may Allah have mercy on him, mentioned these seven of the essential attributes, and among them are oneness in essence and oneness in attributes and self-sufficiency independent of contingent beings and greatness and majesty as has come in the Names and Attributes. Al-Baydawi said: 'Greatness is the opposite of insignificance, and grandeour is the opposite of smallness.' I say: And the High is the opposite of the low, so these are words close in meaning among the Most Beautiful Names, and the statement that they are synonymous arises from dense states; indeed Huijat al-Islam said: It is necessary to believe in a distinction between the meaning of the two words, for it is difficult for us to grasp the difference between their two meanings regarding Allah the Exalted, but nonetheless we do not doubt the fact of distinction. Therefore Allah the Exalted said: 'Majesty is My cloak and Greatness is My wrapper.' So He distinguished between them with a distinction indicating disparity, for each of the cloak and the wrapper is an adornment for a person, but the cloak is nobler than the wrapper, and hence the key to prayer is made the word 'Allahu Akbar'. So these seven are the positive essential attributes, and there is disagreement regarding baqa' as to whether it is among the positive attributes or the negative ones? So some built on the former and collected them in a couplet, saying: 'From life, knowledge, power, will, speech, seeing, hearing, along with baqa'.' And the more apparent is that it is among the negative attributes, for what is meant by it is the negation of prior non-existence and subsequent annihilation, based on the fact that what is established as eternal is impossible to cease, and what is possible to cease is impossible to be eternal. As for what occurs in the text of our master Umar al-Nasafi of his saying: 'The Living, the Powerful, the Knowing, the Hearing, the Seeing, the Wiling, the Intending' – it may lead to the illusion that the wish (mashi'a) and the will (irada) are different, but that is not so, because the discussion on this station has preceded.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the essential attributes of Allah. It begins by stating that the Greatest Imam (Abu Hanifa) listed seven essential attributes, noting that also included are oneness in essence, oneness in attributes, and self-sufficiency, as well as greatness and majesty. The author then quotes al-Baydawi's definitions of 'azm (greatness) and kabir (grandeour) as opposites of smallness, and adds 'ali (high) as opposite of low, noting these are close in meaning among the Beautiful Names. He then addresses the claim that these are mere synonyms, attributing it to dense conditions, and reports Huijat al-Islam (al-Ghazali) stating that one should believe in a distinction between such words, even if the exact difference in Allah's case is difficult. The proof is from a hadith qudsi: 'Majesty is My cloak and Greatness is My wrapper,' where cloak (rida') is nobler than wrapper (izar), and hence 'Allahu Akbar' is the key to prayer, implying greatness is more special. After this, the author confirms that the seven attributes are positive essential attributes, but there is disagreement whether baqa' (eternal persistence) is positive or negative. Some include it as an eighth positive attribute, as in the couplet: 'From life, knowledge, power, will, speech, seeing, hearing, along with baqa'.' However, the author's preferred view is that baqa' is negative, because it negates prior non-existence and future annihilation, based on the principle that what is established eternal cannot cease, and what can cease cannot be eternal. Finally, the author notes that when Mawlana Umar al-Nasafi lists both 'al-shami' (the Wisher) and 'al-murid' (the Willer), it might imply they are different, but they are synonymous, as already clarified.

صفات ثبوتية
الأحدية

الصمدية
العظمة
الكبرياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that attributes like 'azm and kabir are exact synonyms with no difference in meaning.
Correction: The passage argues that there is a real distinction, though difficult to comprehend in relation to Allah, as shown by the hadith qudsi.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the author affirm a distinction between 'azm (greatness) and kabir (majesty)?
A) Because al-Baydawi defined them as opposites of different terms.
B) Because Allah Himself distinguished them in a hadith qudsi by calling majesty His cloak and greatness His wrapper.
C) Because the Imam Abu Hanifa listed them separately.
D) Because they are used in different contexts in prayer.

Key Takeaway
The seven essential attributes are all positive and affirm Allah's perfections, with a real but subtle distinction among terms like greatness and majesty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you mentally list Allah's attributes like greatness and majesty, do you treat them as identical labels or as distinct perfections that deserve separate awe? Which attribute will you deliberately contemplate this week to internalize His grandeur?
?
You just read that the exact difference between attributes is beyond human grasp. Does that humiliate your intellect or liberate it? If it humiliates you, how will you respond in your next prayer to Allah's majesty?
?
In sujud, you say 'Subhana Rabbiyal A'la' (glory to my Lord, the Most High). Do you ever pause to sense the weight of His 'uluw (highness) and 'azama (greatness) in that moment? What will you change today to make that phrase a real acknowledgment of His absolute supremacy?

Is Baqa' a Positive or Negative Attribute?
Discussion on whether baqa' is a positive or negative attribute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement over whether baqa' (eternal persistence) is a positive or negative attribute and why the author prefers the negative view.

Core Idea
Baqa' is best understood as a negative attribute, negating non-existence.

After listing the seven positive attributes, the author turns to the attribute of baqa'. Some scholars add it as an eighth positive attribute, but the author argues that it is actually a negative attribute, meaning the denial of non-existence before and after. In this lesson, we examine both views and the author's reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the disagreement about baqa'
Read the section where the author states the disagreement on whether baqa' is a positive or negative attribute.
Notice that some scholars compiled a couplet including baqa' with the seven.
2
Understand the author's preferred view
The author states that the more apparent view is that baqa' is negative, because it negates prior non-existence and future annihilation.
The principle: what is eternal cannot cease, what can cease cannot be eternal.
3
Note the clarification on mashi'a and irada
The author briefly corrects a potential misunderstanding from al-Nasafi's text that might imply that mashi'a (will) and irada (intention) are different; he says they are the same.
This is a reminder from earlier discussion.

Defining Attributes of Act and the Mu'tazila Position
Definition of attributes of act and Mu'tazila criterion with Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila distinguished attributes of act from attributes of essence based on the criterion of affirmation and negation.

Core Idea
For the Mu'tazila, what can be affirmed or negated of Allah is an attribute of act.

Before diving into the sectarian debates, the author first defines 'attributes of act' as those whose manifestation depends on creation. He then presents the Mu'tazila view, which uses a simple linguistic criterion: if an attribute can be both affirmed and negated of Allah, it belongs to the attributes of act and is therefore created. This lesson examines that criterion and its scriptural support.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of attributes of act
The author defines attributes of act as those that depend on creation for their manifestation. Understand what this means.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila criterion
The Mu'tazila say that attributes that can be both affirmed and negated are attributes of act. Note the examples of creating and providing.
3
Examine the scriptural evidence
The Mu'tazila cite verses where will and speech are affirmed and negated. Observe how they conclude that these attributes are originated.
The verses are from Surah al-Baqarah and an-Nisa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفعلية أي الصفات الفعلية، وهي التي يتوقف ظهورها على وجود الخلق، اعلم أن بين صفات الذات وصفات الفعل مختلف فيه. فعند المعتزلة ما جرى فيه النفي والإثبات فهو من صفات الفعل، كما يقال : خلق لفلان ولداً ولم يخلق لفلان، ورزق لزيد مالاً ولم يرزق لعمرو، وما لا يجري فيه النفي فهو من صفات الذات كالعلم والقدرة فلا يقال : لم يعلم كذا ولم يقدر على كذا فالإرادة والكلام مما يجري فيه النفي والإثبات، قال الله تعالى: يُرِيدُ اللهُ بِكُمُ الْيُسْرَ وَلَا يُرِيدُ بِكُمُ الْعُسْرَ [البقرة: ١٨٥]، ﴿ وَكَلَّمَ اللهُ مُوسى تَكْلِيمًا ﴾ [النساء: ١٦٤]، وَلَا : يُكَلِّمُهُمُ الله يَوْمَ الْقِيِّمَةِ [البقرة: ١٧٤]، فكانا من صفات الفعل وكانا حادثين. وأما عند الأشعرية فالفرق بينهما أن ما يلزم من نفيه نقيضه فهو من صفات الذات، فإنك لو نفيت الحياة يلزم الموت، ولو نفيت القدرة يلزم العجز وكذا العلم مع الجهل، وما لا يلزم من نفيه نقيضه فهو من صفات الفعل، فلو نفيت الإحياء أو الإماتة أو الخلق أو الرزق لم يلزم منه نقيضه، فعلى هذا الحد لو نفيت الإرادة لزم منه الجبر والاضطرار، ولو نفيت عنه الكلام لزم الخرس والسكوت، فثبت أنهما من صفات الذات. وعندنا أن كل ما وصف به ولا يجوز أن يوصف بضده فهو من صفات الذات، كالقدرة والعلم والعزّة والعظمة، وكل ما يجوز أن يوصف به وبضده فهو من صفات الفعل كالرأفة والرحمة والسخط والغضب.
As for the active attributes, i.e., the attributes of act, which are those whose manifestation depends on the existence of creation. Know that there is disagreement regarding the distinction between attributes of essence and attributes of act. According to the Mu'tazila, what admits of affirmation and negation is from the attributes of act, as is said: He created for someone a child and did not create for another, and He provided for Zayd wealth and did not provide for Amr; and what does not admit of affirmation and negation is from the attributes of essence, like knowledge and power, so it is not said: He did not know such-and-such and He was not able to do such-and-such. Thus will and speech are among what admit of affirmation and negation. Allah the Exalted said: 'Allah desires ease for you and does not desire hardship for you' [2:185], and 'And Allah spoke to Moses directly' [4:164], and 'And He will not speak to them on the Day of Resurrection' [2:174]; thus they are from attributes of act and are originated. And according to the Ash'ariyya, the difference between them is that whatever negation entails its contrary is from attributes of essence; for if you negate life, death follows, and if you negate power, incapacity follows, and likewise knowledge with ignorance; and whatever negation does not entail its contrary is from attributes of act; if you negate giving life or causing death or creating or providing, no contrary follows from it. According to this criterion, if you negate will, compulsion and constraint follow, and if you negate speech from Him, dumbness and silence follow; thus it is established that they are from attributes of essence. And according to us: whatever He is described with and it is not permissible to describe Him with its opposite is from attributes of essence, like power, knowledge, might, and greatness; and whatever it is permissible to describe Him with and with its opposite is from attributes of act, like compassion, mercy, displeasure, and anger.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a classification and comparative analysis of the distinction between attributes of essence (sifat al-dhat) and attributes of act (sifat al-fi'l). The author begins by defining attributes of act as those whose manifestation depends on creation. He then presents three positions. First, the Mu'tazila: they say that any attribute that can be affirmed or negated of Allah (like creating, providing, willing, speaking) is an attribute of act, and thus originated (hadith). They support this by noting that we can say 'He created' or 'He did not create', and similarly for will and speech; they cite verses where will and speech are affirmed or negated. Second, the Ash'ariyya hold that the criterion is whether negation necessitates a contrary (did). For example, negating life necessarily implies death, so life is essential; but negating an act like giving life does not imply its opposite (taking life is not a necessary opposite, because one could simply not give life). Applying this to will and speech, negating will implies compulsion (jabr) which is a deficiency, so will is essential; negating speech implies dumbness, so speech is essential. Third, the author states 'according to us' (Maturidi school): the criterion is whether it is permissible to describe Allah by both an attribute and its opposite. If not permissible (e.g., power and inability), it is essential; if permissible (e.g., mercy and anger), it is an attribute of act. Thus, will and speech are essential because one cannot attribute inability or silence to Allah, whereas mercy and anger are active because He can show mercy or anger.

Key Terms
صفات فعلية
صفات ذاتية
المعتزلة
النفي والإثبات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that all attributes that involve creation are attributes of act.
Correction: Mu'tazila specify that only those that can be affirmed and negated are attributes of act; knowledge and power, though also related to creation, are essential because they are never negated.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila, what is the criterion for an attribute being an attribute of act?
A) It is mentioned in the Quran.
B) It can be both affirmed and negated.
C) Its negation implies a deficiency.
D) It only applies to some creatures.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila use the logical test of affirmation/negation to separate created actions from eternal attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that Allah 'does not desire hardship' for you, do you truly believe His will is not a fixed eternal decree but a response to your situation? How does that change the way you seek His help when you feel burdened? Will you tonight acknowledge that His will for you is not set in stone, and ask Him to will ease for you right now?
?
If Allah's speech is an attribute of act—originated in time—does that make the Quran less eternal in your heart? When you open the Quran, do you encounter a timeless message or a fresh act of Allah speaking to you this moment? Commit to reading one verse today as if Allah is speaking it directly to you.
?
You affirm that Allah knows everything—an eternal attribute. But do you live as though His knowledge of your sin is a fact that cannot change? Or do you realize that He also wills to forgive you, as an act He chooses? Which way of thinking leads you to more hope? Before you sleep, repent to Allah as if His forgiveness is a new act He is about to perform—because it is.
